### [311] [Reasoning with Sampling: Cutting at Decision Points](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30327)
*Felix Zhou,Anay Mehrotra,Quanquan C. Liu*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Entropy-Cut Metropolis-Hastings: A new sampling algorithm that identifies key decision points in reasoning traces using next-token entropy, enabling more efficient sampling from power distributions for reasoning tasks without requiring RL training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While recent work showed that sampling from power distributions of base language models can achieve reasoning comparable to RL-trained models, existing samplers are inefficient because they uniformly select cut positions, often rewriting local details instead of revisiting crucial decision points like proof strategies or algorithm choices.

Method: The authors introduce Entropy-Cut Metropolis-Hastings, which uses the base model's next-token entropy as a proxy to identify key decision points. By focusing resampling on positions with entropy jumps (indicating consequential decisions), the algorithm better mixes to the power distribution by revisiting different reasoning strategies rather than tweaking minor details.

Result: Empirical verification shows entropy jumps are useful proxies for decision points. Theoretical analysis proves the method's mixing time scales with the number of decisions rather than tokens (which can be much larger). Across MATH500, HumanEval, GPQA Diamond, and AIME26, the method consistently outperforms baselines and RL-trained models.

Conclusion: The proposed entropy-based sampling method provides an efficient, training-free alternative to RL posttraining for reasoning tasks by better targeting consequential decision points, with both theoretical guarantees and empirical improvements across diverse reasoning benchmarks.

Abstract: Frontier reasoning models are produced by posttraining base language models with reinforcement learning. Recent work has challenged this by showing that sampling from a sharpened version of the base model's distribution, a so-called power distribution, elicits comparable reasoning without additional training, curated datasets, or verifiers. However, making this method practical requires efficiently sampling from the power distribution. A sampler needs to "mix" to the power distribution, which necessitates moving between modes of the target distribution; intuitively, e.g., trying different reasoning strategies. The samplers proposed in prior works repeatedly select a "cut" position in the current reasoning trace uniformly at random and resample the suffix from that position onward. However, reasoning traces typically contain a few consequential decisions (e.g., the choice of proof strategy or algorithm), and we observe that a uniformly chosen cut tends to rewrite local details rather than revisit decision points. We introduce an algorithm (Entropy-Cut Metropolis-Hastings) that uses the base model's next-token entropy as a proxy to identify key decision points and resample from those positions. We empirically verify that entropy jumps are a useful proxy for decision points and, in a stylized model of reasoning, prove that our method's mixing time scales with the number of decisions in a trace rather than with the number of tokens, which can be much larger. Across MATH500, HumanEval, GPQA Diamond, and AIME26, our method consistently improves over baselines and RL-trained models.

### [312] [SoundnessBench: Can Your AI Scientist Really Tell Good Research Ideas from Bad Ones?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30329)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Created SoundnessBench - a curated benchmark of 1,099 machine-learning research proposals reconstructed from ICLR submissions, labeled with reviewer soundness sub-scores, and audited against source papers. Tested 12 frontier LLMs under standard and aggressive prompting conditions.

Result: Found pervasive optimism bias: under standard prompting, models frequently rate low-soundness proposals as sound. Aggressive prompting largely shifts errors from false positives to false negatives. Additional controls for various confounders suggest this behavior isn't explained by a single factor.

Conclusion: Current LLMs are not yet reliable as standalone first-gate evaluators for scientific rigor in research proposals, despite the need for such capability in autonomous AI research systems.

Abstract: Autonomous AI research agents aim to accelerate scientific discovery by automating the research pipeline, from hypothesis generation to peer review. However, existing benchmarks rarely test a fundamental bottleneck: whether Large Language Models can judge the methodological viability of a research idea before expending time and computational resources. We introduce SoundnessBench, a curated benchmark of 1,099 machine-learning research proposals reconstructed from ICLR submissions, labeled with reviewer soundness sub-scores, and audited against source papers. SoundnessBench should be interpreted as a benchmark for recoverable proposal-stage soundness rather than exact prediction of full-paper review outcomes. Across 12 frontier LLMs, we find a pervasive optimism bias: under standard prompting, models frequently rate low-soundness proposals as sound, while aggressive prompting largely shifts errors from false positives to false negatives. Additional controls for public-corpus contamination, paper-identifying phrases, surface features, and human audit quality suggest that this behavior is not explained by a single confounder. Our results indicate that current LLMs are not yet reliable as standalone first-gate evaluators for scientific rigor.

### [313] [When, why, and how do diffusion posterior samplers fail? A finite-sample lens](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30330)
*Benjamin A. Burns,Sara Fridovich-Keil*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper analyzes approximation errors in diffusion model-based posterior sampling for imaging inverse problems, showing how likelihood approximations at intermediate timesteps can distort posterior distributions, and introduces a finite-sample diagnostic framework to evaluate these errors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion models are popular for posterior sampling in imaging inverse problems, but existing methods must use inexact approximations for likelihood at intermediate timesteps for computational tractability. While these approximations often work empirically, their downstream effects on sampled posteriors are poorly understood and can lead to unexplained failures.

Method: The authors introduce a finite-sample perspective on posterior sampling that approximates the posterior to arbitrary precision as training set size tends towards infinity. This approach works for any forward model and prior distribution, and serves as a drop-in diagnostic tool to evaluate accuracy of posterior samplers.

Result: Popular posterior sampling approximations tend to under- or over-estimate posterior spread at intermediate timesteps, causing downstream issues including sensitivity to early stopping time, inaccurate weighting of posterior modes, and hallucinations of prior modes not in posterior or likelihood modes not supported by prior. These errors can arise even with linear measurement models or unimodal posteriors, just from multimodal priors and inaccurate posterior spread estimates.

Conclusion: The finite-sample posterior sampling approach provides a diagnostic framework to understand when, why, and how likelihood approximations propagate to erroneous posterior distributions. It's agnostic to likelihood approximation type and forward model type, making it broadly applicable for evaluating accuracy and failure modes of existing and future posterior samplers.

Abstract: Diffusion models have excellent capacity to model complex distributions of natural data, which has made them a popular and effective choice for posterior sampling in imaging inverse problems. Existing methods can incorporate any measurement model at inference time but must use an inexact approximation for the likelihood at intermediate timesteps for computational tractability. Although these approximations can often work well empirically, their downstream effect on the sampled posterior is poorly understood and can result in unexplained failures. To understand when, why, and how these likelihood approximations propagate to erroneous posterior distributions, we introduce a finite-sample perspective on posterior sampling that approximates the posterior to arbitrary precision as training set size tends towards infinity, for any forward model and prior distribution. Using this finite-sample lens, we observe that popular posterior sampling approximations tend to under- or over-estimate the spread of the posterior at intermediate timesteps, causing downstream consequences including sensitivity to early stopping time, inaccurate relative weighting of posterior modes, and hallucination, both of prior modes that are not in the posterior and likelihood modes that are not supported by the prior. Moreover, we find that the cause of these posterior errors requires neither a nonlinear measurement model nor a multimodal posterior, but can arise solely due to a multimodal prior and inaccurate posterior spread at intermediate sampling times. Our finite-sample posterior sampling approach is agnostic to the type of likelihood approximation and the type of (linear or nonlinear) forward model, and can thus serve as a drop-in diagnostic to evaluate the accuracy and failure modes of existing and future posterior samplers.

</details>


### [314] [Fairness-Aware Federated Learning with Trajectory Shapley Value](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30336)
*Daniel Kuznetsov,Ziqi Wang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conventional federated learning aggregation schemes use fixed weights that fail to reflect unequal and time-varying client contributions, leading to biased and unstable learning. There is a need to improve fairness and stability in federated optimization.

Method: The authors propose FedTSV, an adaptive aggregation method that uses Trajectory Shapley Value (TSV) to evaluate client contributions based on their influence on the global model's optimization trajectory using a validation-based, temporally consistent utility. TSV evaluations are converted into dynamic client weights for real-time aggregation.

Result: Experiments on benchmark datasets show that FedTSV accelerates convergence, improves robustness, and yields more equitable contribution assessments compared to conventional aggregation methods.

Conclusion: FedTSV provides a principled foundation for fairness-aware federated optimization by accelerating convergence, improving robustness, and enabling more equitable contribution assessments through its adaptive aggregation based on Trajectory Shapley Value.

Abstract: Federated learning is an emerging distributed paradigm that addresses the challenges posed by heterogeneous, privacy-sensitive data. It enables multiple clients to train a model collaboratively by aggregating their local updates at a server. However, conventional aggregation schemes typically use fixed weights that fail to reflect unequal and time-varying client contributions, leading to biased and unstable learning. To improve fairness and stability, we propose the Trajectory Shapley Value (TSV), a contribution metric that evaluates how each client influences the optimization trajectory of the global model using a validation-based, temporally consistent utility. Building on TSV, we design FedTSV, an adaptive aggregation method that converts per-round evaluations into dynamic client weights, allowing the server to respond to heterogeneous and adversarial participation in real time. Experiments on benchmark datasets show that FedTSV accelerates convergence, improves robustness, and yields more equitable contribution assessments, thereby providing a principled foundation for fairness-aware federated optimization.

### [315] [Efficient Test-Time Finetuning of LLMs via Convex Reconstruction and Gradient Caching](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30337)
*Alaa Khamis,Alaa Maalouf*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: HullFT is a geometric TTFT method that uses sparse convex combinations of training sequences for efficient retrieval and exploits gradient reuse for faster finetuning, achieving better quality-efficiency tradeoffs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current test-time finetuning (TTFT) methods face a fundamental speed-quality tradeoff: fast retrieval methods often select redundant sequences, while diversity-aware selection methods add prohibitive per-query computational costs, making TTFT impractical for real-world applications.

Method: 1) Represent query embedding as sparse convex combination of few training sequences using Frank-Wolfe optimization; 2) Convert fractional convex weights into exact integer multiset via geometric integerization; 3) Exploit repeated examples with Gradient Reuse to amortize forward-backward computation across finetuning steps.

Result: HullFT improves the quality-efficiency tradeoff over state-of-the-art TTFT methods, achieving lower bits-per-byte (better performance) at substantially lower total runtime compared to existing approaches.

Conclusion: The geometric approach of HullFT addresses both retrieval and finetuning bottlenecks in TTFT through sparse convex combinations and gradient reuse, making test-time finetuning more practical by simultaneously improving quality and reducing computational costs.

Abstract: Test-time finetuning (TTFT) is a rapidly evolving paradigm that adapts a language model to each prompt by retrieving related sequences, updating the model on them, and then evaluating the prompt. However, TTFT is only practical if it is fast: selection and finetuning both happen per query, making each a direct bottleneck. Existing methods trade speed for quality: fast retrieval is often redundant, while stronger diversity-aware selection adds prohibitive per-query cost. We introduce HullFT, a geometric approach to TTFT that addresses both bottlenecks. Given a query, HullFT first represents the query embedding as a sparse convex combination of few training sequences, using efficient projection-free Frank-Wolfe optimization. This yields a support set that is inherently relevant and diverse. We then convert the fractional convex weights into an exact integer multiset for finetuning through a geometric integerization procedure. The resulting multiplicities naturally create repeated examples, which we exploit with Gradient Reuse to amortize forward-backward computation across repeated finetuning steps. Our experiments show that HullFT improves the quality-efficiency tradeoff over current state-of-the-art TTFT methods, achieving lower bits-per-byte at substantially lower total runtime.

</details>

### [316] [The incremental voter model: mean-field analysis and convergence to equilibrium](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28984)
*Fei Cao,Xiaoqian Gong*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: The incremental voter model (IVM) is a discrete-opinion agent-based system where agents make small, biased opinion changes toward a persuader's opinion, with analysis revealing polarization patterns through mean-field equations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To develop a mathematical framework for understanding social influence processes, particularly opinion polarization, where agents make incremental changes rather than abrupt opinion switches, better modeling real-world gradual opinion shifts.

Method: Developed an agent-based model where agents with discrete opinions in range {-k,...,k} interact randomly; a listener updates opinion by at most one unit toward persuader's opinion. Derived mean-field nonlinear ODEs for large-population limit to analyze asymptotic behavior mathematically.

Result: Established rigorous mathematical framework using mean-field equations to study opinion distribution in large-population limit, enabling analysis of polarization dynamics and asymptotic behavior of the system.

Conclusion: The incremental voter model provides deeper understanding of social influence processes in complex systems, particularly for modeling opinion polarization, and serves as foundation for developing more advanced future models.

Abstract: We introduce the incremental voter model (IVM), a discrete-opinion multi-agent system where agents undergo step-wise transitions biased by the opinion of a randomly selected persuader. Our incremental voter model comprises a large population of interacting agents, each holding an opinion represented by an element of the discrete set $\{-k,\ldots,0,\ldots,k\}, k \in \mathbb{N}_{+}$. At each update step as time progresses, a pair of distinct agents are selected independently and uniformly at random from the population, and the first agent (viewed as the ``listener'') updates its opinion based on that of the second (viewed as the ``persuader''), adopting a new opinion that differs from its current one by at most one unit. By deriving the mean-field system of nonlinear ordinary differential equations (ODEs) that governs the large-population limit of the agent-based model, we develop a rigorous mathematical framework to study the asymptotic behavior of the opinion distribution in the mean-field limit. These results contribute to a deeper understanding of social influence processes in complex systems, particularly in modeling opinion polarization, and may guide the formulation of more advanced models in future research.

</details>


### [317] [LLM-ALSO: LLM-Driven Adaptive Learning-Signal Optimization for Multi-Agent Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.29293)
*Xiaoguang Wu,Zhi Zheng,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: LLM-ALSO is an iterative LLM-driven framework that adaptively optimizes learning signals for multi-agent reinforcement learning in sparse-reward settings through diagnosis, proposal, and validation cycles.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current MARL training guidance struggles in sparse-reward settings where weak supervision limits coordination and policy improvement. Existing methods require substantial domain expertise or manual design, and current LLM-based approaches are largely single-agent-oriented, one-shot, or not well-validated for cooperative MARL's evolving training dynamics.

Method: LLM-ALSO uses an iterative three-step process: 1) Critic LLM diagnoses stage-specific learning and coordination failures from sparse-return metrics and behavior evidence, 2) Generator LLM proposes candidate reward-shaping configurations based on diagnosis, and 3) branch-validation feedback refines candidates before affecting main training. The framework includes short-horizon validation and stage-aware adaptation to ensure only validated updates influence training.

Result: Experiments on sparse-reward cooperative MARL tasks demonstrate that LLM-ALSO improves sparse-evaluation performance and learning efficiency compared to existing approaches.

Conclusion: LLM-ALSO provides an effective framework for adaptive learning-signal optimization in MARL, leveraging LLMs' flexibility while mitigating risks of unreliable LLM-generated modifications through systematic validation and stage-aware adaptation.

Abstract: Effective training-time guidance is central to multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL), yet remains difficult in sparse-reward settings where weak supervision limits coordination and policy improvement, and existing methods often require substantial domain expertise or manual design effort. Large language models (LLMs) provide a promising alternative for flexible learning-signal design, yet existing LLM-based methods remain largely single-agent-oriented, one-shot, or weakly validated for the evolving training dynamics of cooperative MARL. To address these limitations, we propose LLM-ALSO, an iterative LLM-driven adaptive learning-signal optimization framework for MARL. Rather than directly deploying LLM-generated rewards, LLM-ALSO decomposes adaptation into iterative diagnosis, proposal, and validation: a Critic LLM diagnoses stage-specific learning and coordination failures from sparse-return metrics and compact behavior evidence, a Generator LLM proposes candidate reward-shaping configurations conditioned on the diagnosis, and branch-validation feedback refines candidates before they affect the main training trajectory. Through short-horizon validation and stage-aware adaptation, LLM-ALSO promotes only validated updates into training, reducing the risk of unreliable LLM-generated modifications. Experiments on sparse-reward cooperative MARL tasks show that LLM-ALSO improves sparse-evaluation performance and learning efficiency.

### [318] [DynaGraph: Lightweight Multi-Model Interaction Framework via Dynamic Topological Reconfiguration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.29511)
*Yanxing Guo,Zihao Zheng,Fangzhou Wu,Ling Liang,Lin Bao,Zongwei Wang,Yimao Cai*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: DynaGraph is a lightweight multi-model framework that uses dynamic topological reconfiguration and PEFT adapters to achieve reasoning capabilities comparable to much larger models while reducing computational overhead significantly.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional approaches face critical dilemmas: massive monolithic LLMs have severe computational redundancy, while task decomposition methods either use predefined static topologies (vulnerable to cascading errors) or unconstrained dynamic agents (suffer from trajectory divergence and unpredictable memory bloat).

Method: DynaGraph uses dynamic topological reconfiguration with two levels: (1) Execution level: time-division PEFT adapters multiplexed over a shared base model, enabling full system training/inference on a single consumer GPU; (2) Routing level: Evaluator monitors execution confidence to trigger hierarchical self-healing (Fine-grained Patching for localized data gaps, Subgraph Reconstruction for severe logical ruptures).

Result: On StrategyQA, MATH, and FinQA, their 8B model closely approximates reasoning capabilities of a 72B monolithic model (87.6% on StrategyQA, 82.7% on MATH), while reducing latency by up to 68.1% and token consumption by 68.6% compared to unconstrained dynamic architectures.

Conclusion: DynaGraph successfully addresses the computational redundancy of monolithic LLMs and the limitations of static/decomposed approaches by introducing dynamic topological reconfiguration with efficient parameter usage, achieving state-of-the-art performance with significantly reduced resource requirements.

Abstract: Tackling complex reasoning tasks typically relies on massive monolithic LLMs, which suffer from severe computational redundancy. While task decomposition through structured pipelines or multi-agent collaborations offers an alternative, these approaches inevitably fall into a critical dilemma: predefined static topologies are highly vulnerable to cascading errors, whereas unconstrained dynamic agents suffer from trajectory divergence and unpredictable memory bloat. To address this, we present DynaGraph, a lightweight multi-model framework driven by dynamic topological reconfiguration. At the execution level, DynaGraph multiplexes time-division PEFT adapters over a shared base model, enabling both full system training and inference deployment on a single consumer-grade GPU. At the routing level, the Evaluator continuously monitors execution confidence to trigger hierarchical self-healing: Fine-grained Patching for localized data gaps and Subgraph Reconstruction for severe logical ruptures. Experiments on StrategyQA, MATH, and FinQA demonstrate our 8B model closely approximates the reasoning capabilities of a 72B monolithic model (e.g., 87.6% on StrategyQA, 82.7% on MATH). Furthermore, it reduces latency by up to 68.1% and token consumption by 68.6% compared to unconstrained dynamic architectures.

### [319] [CONCAT: Consensus- and Confidence-Driven Ad Hoc Teaming for Efficient LLM-Based Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.29612)
*Ziyang Ma,Dingyi Zhang,Sichu Liang,Jiajia Chu,Pengfei Xia,Hui Zang,Deyu Zhou*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: CONCAT is a training-free multi-agent collaboration framework that uses consensus-based clustering and confidence-driven leader selection to efficiently organize LLM agent interactions, reducing communication overhead while maintaining performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based multi-agent systems show superior performance on complex tasks but suffer from huge computational overhead due to heavy agent communication. Existing solutions require extra training which introduces costs and limits generalizability to specific domains.

Method: A training-free framework that clusters agents by initial answers, selects leaders based on confidence, uses Theory of Mind-based heuristic to predict collaboration benefits, and organizes an ad hoc network by pruning communications with low predicted benefits.

Result: Achieves up to 2.02x higher efficiency (accuracy/latency ratio) than LLM-Debate, outperforms training-aware methods like AgentDropout, reduces average latency by 50.1% on Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct across three LLMs and benchmarks, with no task-specific training.

Conclusion: CONCAT provides an effective training-free solution for efficient multi-agent collaboration that reduces communication overhead while maintaining performance, offering better efficiency and generalizability compared to both training-free and training-aware alternatives.

Abstract: Although large language model (LLM) based multi-agent systems (MAS) show their capability to solve complex tasks and achieve higher performance over single agent systems, they lead to huge computational overheads because of heavy communication between agents. Previous research has made efforts to train a sparse multi-agent graph or fine-tune a planner to orchestrate the workflow better. However, such extra training processes introduce computational costs and limit MAS to specific domains, therefore compromising their generalizability. In this paper, we propose CONCAT, a training-free multi-agent collaboration framework based on CONsensus and Confidence-driven Ad hoc Teaming to efficiently organize agent interactions. Specifically, agents are clustered based on their initial answers, and leaders of each cluster are selected based on the agents' confidence. Then, a heuristic function based on the Theory of Mind is designed to predict the collaboration benefits between every two leaders according to their answers and confidence. Finally, an ad hoc multi-agent network is organized after evicting a percentage of communications based on the predicted benefits. Experiments across three LLMs and three benchmarks show that CONCAT achieves up to 2.02x higher efficiency (accuracy/latency ratio) than LLM-Debate and outperforms training-aware methods such as AgentDropout, while reducing average latency by 50.1% on Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct, without any task-specific training.

### [320] [Evolve as a Team: Collaborative Self-Evolution for LLM-based Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.29790)

Result: Meta-Team consistently outperforms single-agent systems, hand-crafted MAS, and prior MAS evolution methods across six long-horizon agent benchmarks. Further analyses show Meta-Team enables more reliable and scalable MAS self-evolution.

Abstract: LLM-based multi-agent systems (MAS) have emerged as an effective paradigm for complex and long-horizon tasks. However, in real-world tasks, MAS often exhibit various failures during execution and such failures are difficult to eliminate during design. This motivates experience-driven MAS evolution, where a system improves based on its own execution experience. Yet such evolution is challenging because MAS experience is prolonged and intricate, interleaving multiple agents' execution chains and communication messages, which makes it difficult to identify what should be improved. To address this challenge, we propose Meta-Team, an experience-driven MAS evolution framework based on collaborative self-evolution. Meta-Team preserves the execution context of each agent and coordinates post-task communication, enabling agents to exchange distributed evidence for evolution. Building on this design, Meta-Team conducts multi-scale self-evolution, transforming execution experience into reusable improvements to agent behaviors, inter-agent coordination, and team-level organization. Across six long-horizon agent benchmarks, Meta-Team consistently outperforms single-agent systems, hand-crafted MAS, and prior MAS evolution methods; further analyses demonstrate that Meta-Team enables more reliable and scalable MAS self-evolution.

### [321] [Evolutionary Dynamics of Cooperation in Next-Generation LLM Agent Systems: A Cross-Provider Empirical Extension](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.29874)
*Francisco León Zúñiga Bolívar*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: Cooperative bias persists in 2025-2026 frontier LLMs despite provider diversity; cross-provider divergence is substantial; noise remains challenging; provider identity matters more than model generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To determine if next-generation LLM agents inherit the cooperative biases documented in earlier models (ChatGPT-4o, Claude 3.5 Sonnet), or if scale and provider diversity reshape equilibrium behavior in competitive multi-agent settings.

Method: Extended Willis et al.'s evolutionary game theory benchmark to four 2025-2026 frontier models (Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 2.5 Flash, Gemini 3.1 Pro, GPT-5.4 Mini) using the Iterated Prisoner's Dilemma. Applied identical protocol across three prompting styles (Default, Prose, Self-Refine) and four population compositions (balanced/biased with/without noise). Conducted n=500 Moran iterations per condition.

Result: 1. Cooperative bias persists across providers (H1: confirmed) - 9/12 model-prompt combinations favor cooperative equilibria. 2. Partial support for aggressive capability parity (H2) - Self-Refine raises ICD in all models; Claude Sonnet 4.6 Refine achieved highest ICD (0.913). 3. Substantial cross-provider divergence (H3: confirmed) - Gemini 2.5 Flash reached 77% aggressive equilibria under biased conditions; GPT-5.4 Mini reached 70% cooperative equilibria under Self-Refine. 4. Directionally positive but not robustly confirmed noise robustness (H4) - average noise sensitivity decreased but not statistically significant.

Conclusion: Provider identity is the strongest correlate of equilibrium outcomes in LLM agent behavior, not model generation, and noise remains a universal challenge across all models regardless of size or age.

Abstract: Do next-generation LLM agents inherit the cooperative biases documented in their predecessors, or does scale and provider diversity reshape equilibrium behaviour in competitive multi-agent settings? Willis et al. established a benchmark for this question using evolutionary game theory and the Iterated Prisoner's Dilemma (IPD), finding consistent cooperative biases in ChatGPT-4o and Claude 3.5 Sonnet. We extend this benchmark to four frontier models released in 2025-2026 - Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 2.5 Flash, Gemini 3.1 Pro, and GPT-5.4 Mini - applying the identical protocol across three prompting styles (Default, Prose, Self-Refine) and four population compositions (balanced and biased, with and without noise). Cooperative bias persists across providers (H1): nine of twelve model-prompt combinations favour cooperative equilibria in balanced noiseless conditions. Cross-provider divergence is substantial (H3): Gemini 2.5 Flash reaches up to 77% aggressive equilibria under biased conditions, while GPT-5.4 Mini reaches 70% cooperative equilibria under Self-Refine. Support for aggressive capability parity is partial (H2): Self-Refine raises ICD in all models and Claude Sonnet 4.6 Refine achieves the highest ICD in the dataset (0.913), but Default and Prose prompts show no systematic narrowing. Evidence on noise robustness is directionally positive but not robustly confirmed (H4): with n=500 Moran iterations per condition, average noise sensitivity is approximately 6 percentage points for Claude Sonnet 4.6 versus 13 pp for Claude 3.5 Sonnet, but this cross-study gap is not statistically significant once the predecessor's unreported sampling error is propagated. Provider identity, rather than model generation, is the strongest correlate of equilibrium outcomes; noise remains a universal challenge regardless of model size or vintage.

</details>


### [322] [Discovering Cooperative Pipelines: Autoresearch for Sequential Social Dilemmas](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30003)
*Víctor Gallego*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: An AI researcher agent autonomously redesigns LLM policy-synthesis pipelines for multi-agent cooperation games, outperforming baselines and revealing objective-dependent design choices, particularly fairness mechanisms for Rawlsian welfare.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To develop an automated approach for designing effective AI cooperation pipelines in multi-agent social dilemmas, overcoming limitations of hand-designed systems and simple prompt optimization.

Method: A two-level autoresearch framework with an outer-loop AI researcher agent (R) that autonomously redesigns the inner-loop pipeline of an LLM policy-synthesis system for multi-agent Sequential Social Dilemmas (SSDs). The researcher reads source code, edits system prompts, feedback functions, helper libraries, iteration logic, runs evaluations, and decides what to keep.

Result: The researcher agent reliably exceeds hand-designed baselines, sharply reduces run-to-run variance, and outperforms prompt-only optimization. Discovered pipelines are objective-dependent - under Rawlsian maximin, the researcher injects explicit fairness mechanisms absent in efficiency-optimized pipelines, supporting an information-design interpretation.

Conclusion: The autoresearch framework successfully creates tailored AI pipelines for different welfare objectives through automated iterative improvement, revealing that objective-dependent information revelation to bounded rational agents is an effective strategy in social dilemma cooperation problems.

Abstract: We study two-level autoresearch for cooperation: an outer-loop AI agent autonomously redesigns the inner-loop pipeline of an LLM policy-synthesis system for multi-agent Sequential Social Dilemmas (SSDs). A researcher agent $\mathcal{R}$ (run as a coding agent) reads the inner-loop source code, edits system prompts, feedback functions, helper libraries, and iteration logic, runs evaluations, and decides what to keep, following the autoresearch paradigm. Across two games (Cleanup and Gathering), two policy-synthesizer LLMs, and two welfare objectives (utilitarian efficiency and Rawlsian maximin), the researcher reliably exceeds hand-designed baselines, sharply tightens run-to-run variance, and outperforms prompt-only optimization. The discovered pipelines are objective-dependent: only under maximin does the researcher inject an explicit fairness mechanism into synthesizer pipelines, a class of mechanism that is absent from its own objective-agnostic system prompt and from every efficiency-optimized pipeline. This supports an information-design reading in which the researcher chooses what to reveal to the boundedly rational synthesizer as a function of the welfare objective. Code at https://github.com/vicgalle/autoresearch-social-dilemmas.

</details>


### [323] [When Cloud Agents Meet Device Agents: Lessons from Hybrid Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30102)
*Corrado Rainone,Davide Belli,Bence Major,Arash Behboodi*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: Systematic analysis of hybrid multi-agent systems reveals that optimal design is task-specific and more compute doesn't guarantee better performance, though small models can benefit from large model assistance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hybrid multi-agent systems combining on-device small language models and cloud-based large language models offer a promising middle ground between performance and cost, but the design space is complex and poorly understood. Current implementations use ad hoc decisions without general design principles.

Method: The authors adapt two representative multi-agent system architectures to support hybrid inference and systematically study how design choices affect the trade-offs between power consumption, monetary cost, and task performance. They examine how these choices shift the operating point along the Pareto frontier.

Result: The study reveals nuanced findings: while SLMs can effectively benefit from LLM assistance, the optimal architecture depends heavily on the specific task. Greater computational resources (frontier-level compute) don't consistently translate to better performance across all scenarios.

Conclusion: While small language models can benefit from large language model assistance, the optimal hybrid multi-agent system architecture is highly task-dependent, and more compute power doesn't always lead to better performance. There are no universal design principles for hybrid MAS systems.

Abstract: The design space of agentic AI inference spans two extremes: frontier large language models (LLMs), typically hosted in the cloud and offering strong performance across a wide range of tasks at substantially high cost, and more cost-efficient small language models (SLMs), which are amenable to on-device inference. Hybrid multi-agent systems (MASs) combining on-device and cloud models offer a promising middle ground, but they also introduce a complex and poorly understood design space in which task accuracy, monetary cost, and edge energy consumption are tightly coupled; in the absence of general design principles, hybrid components, although not the most prevalent choice, are typically introduced through ad hoc decisions tailored to specific domains. In this work, we examine this design space more systematically. We adapt two representative MAS architectures to support hybrid inference and study how individual design choices shift the operating point along the Pareto frontier of power, cost, and performance. Our findings paint a nuanced picture of hybrid MAS design: while SLMs can effectively benefit from LLM assistance, the optimal architecture is highly task-dependent, and greater frontier-level compute does not consistently translate to better performance.

### [324] [Unifying Temporal and Structural Credit Assignment in LLM-Based Multi-Agent Prompt Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30227)
*Wenwu Li,Yuran Song,Mingze Zhao,Bo Jin,Wenhao Li*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: A new framework for optimizing multi-agent LLM systems using temporal/structural credit assignment and verbalized block coordinate descent that improves performance while reducing query costs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper addresses the challenge of optimizing Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) for large language models, where existing black-box optimizers struggle due to discrete, non-differentiable computation graphs and sparse global supervisory signals. Current methods inefficiently attribute trajectory-level failures to specific local components, leading to high-variance exploration.

Method: The authors introduce temporal and structural credit assignment techniques combined with a discrete verbalized block coordinate descent algorithm. Temporal credit uses state-space bottlenecks to identify critical rounds, while structural credit employs stationary role policies to isolate agent contributions. The algorithm alternates between optimizing role prompts and aggregation protocols using LLM-generated "proxy gradients" to target identified weak links.

Result: Across diverse reasoning benchmarks, the proposed approach substantially reduces query complexity while improving performance compared to existing methods.

Conclusion: The paper proposes a principled and interpretable approach for self-improving multi-agent systems that addresses key challenges in MAS optimization through structural inductive biases and targeted refinement strategies.

Abstract: While Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) empower Large Language Models to tackle complex reasoning tasks through collaborative interaction, optimizing their dynamics remains a formidable challenge due to the discrete, non-differentiable nature of the computation graph and the sparsity of global supervisory signals. Existing black-box optimizers struggle to attribute trajectory-level failure to specific local components, resulting in inefficient, high-variance exploration. We argue that tractable MAS optimization needs structural inductive biases to disentangle error signals. We propose temporal and structural credit assignment, which decomposes the objective along two axes: (i) temporal credit, using state-space bottlenecks to identify critical rounds, and (ii) structural credit, using stationary role policies to isolate agent contributions. Leveraging these decomposed signals, we introduce a discrete, verbalized block coordinate descent algorithm for iterative refinement. Rather than indiscriminate global updates, it alternates between optimizing role prompts and aggregation protocols, using LLM-generated "proxy gradients" to target only the identified weak links. Across diverse reasoning benchmarks, our approach substantially reduces query complexity while improving performance, providing a principled and interpretable path toward self-improving MAS.

</details>

### [326] [SpecBench: Evaluating Specification-Level Reasoning for Software Engineering LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.30314)
*Grant Hamblin,Kevin Song,Zhanda Zhu,Anand Jayarajan,Sihang Liu,Nandita Vijaykumar,Gennady Pekhimenko*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: SpecBench introduces a new benchmark to evaluate SWE agents' ability to identify specification deficiencies (omissions, ambiguities, inconsistencies, errors) in design proposals, moving beyond just code generation to assess full specification-level reasoning using real-world RFC processes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current SWE benchmarks (like SWE-Bench) focus only on code implementation given fixed, precise requirements, assuming specifications are already correct and complete. However, in real-world complex software systems, initial specifications are often incomplete and flawed, requiring extensive expert review and revision before implementation - a critical gap that existing benchmarks don't address.

Method: 1. Derive tasks from real Request for Comments (RFC) processes of mature open-source projects
2. Provide agents with: initial design proposal, project codebase, and past RFC discussions
3. Task: Identify specification deficiencies (omissions, ambiguities, inconsistencies, incorrect assumptions)
4. Evaluate predictions against expert maintainers' historical critiques from actual RFC reviews
5. Include tasks from 5 diverse repositories: Kubernetes, React, Rust, TVM, and vLLM

Result: The best performing agent, GPT-5.4, achieves 44.4% accuracy on SpecBench tasks, indicating significant room for improvement in specification-level reasoning compared to human expert reviewers.

Conclusion: SpecBench fills a critical gap in evaluating SWE agents by focusing on specification design review - an essential phase in software development lifecycle where initial proposals are refined through expert review before implementation. The benchmark reveals current agents' limitations in specification-level reasoning without execution feedback.

Abstract: Software engineering (SWE) agents are transitioning from code generation to full software development lifecycle automation. A critical phase in this lifecycle is specification design: transforming initial proposals into carefully considered requirements through expert review. Existing benchmarks such as SWE-Bench are implementation-focused by measuring the agent's ability to generate code given fixed, precise design requirements. This formulation assumes specifications are correct and complete. In real-world complex and critical software systems, initial specifications are often incomplete and flawed, requiring extensive expert reviews and revisions before being accepted for implementation. To fill this gap, we introduce SpecBench to evaluate specification-level reasoning: the ability to generate complete, unambiguous, consistent, and correct system specifications. SpecBench tasks are derived from the Request for Comments (RFC) process used by mature open-source projects. For each task, an agent is given an initial design proposal, the project codebase, and all past project RFC discussions. The agent is tasked with identifying specification deficiencies: omissions, ambiguities, inconsistencies, or incorrect assumptions in the initial proposal. We evaluate predictions against critiques raised by expert maintainers during historical RFC reviews. SpecBench contains tasks from 5 diverse repositories: Kubernetes, React, Rust, TVM, and vLLM. We evaluate state-of-the-art SWE agents on SpecBench, analyzing their capacity to reason about system design without execution feedback. The best performing agent, GPT-5.4, achieves 44.4% accuracy.

</details>
